Title: Tests for Independence of High-Dimensional Nonstationary Time Series

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.08498v1

Published Time: Tue, 09 Jun 2026 01:45:12 GMT

Number of Pages: 95

Markdown Content:
# Tests for Independence of High-Dimensional Nonstationary Time Series 

## Yunyi Zhang School of Data Science, The Chinese University of Hong Kong, Shenzhen, Shenzhen, Guangdong 518172, China zhangyunyi@cuhk.edu.cn June 9, 2026 

Abstract 

This manuscript studies the problem of independence testing between two high-dimensional time series without assuming weak stationarity, allowing their autoco-variances to vary over time. To this end, we propose a bimodal weighted-average test statistic that removes the bias induced by temporal dependence under the null hypothesis, thereby avoiding the need to whiten the time series prior to hypothe-sis testingвҖ”a procedure that is challenging in high-dimensional and nonstationary settings. To facilitate statistical inference, we develop a dependent wild bootstrap procedure. On the theoretical side, we derive a concentration inequality for quadratic forms of time series data stemming from a class of high-dimensional, nonlinear, and nonstationary processes. This result enables us to derive the asymptotic null distri-bution of the proposed test statistic and to establish the validity of the bootstrap algorithm. Numerical results show that the proposed test attains desired size and good power performance even when the dimension exceeds the sample size or when the data-generating process exhibits time-varying autocovariances. In contrast, tests based on whitening time series fail to maintain correct size in the presence of unstable autocovariance structures. Since nonstationary autocovariances commonly arise in real-life time series data, our work offers a robust procedure for independence testing. 

Keywords: Independence Test; Nonstationary time series; High-dimensional data; Quadratic form; Wild bootstrap. 1

> arXiv:2606.08498v1 [math.ST] 7 Jun 2026

# 1 Introduction 

## 1.1 Overview 

Suppose that we observe a mean-zero, vector-valued time series zрқ‘Ў = ( xвҠӨрқ‘Ў , yвҠӨрқ‘Ў )вҠӨ вҲҲ Rрқ‘‘ 1+рқ‘‘ 2

for рқ‘Ў = 1, 2, вӢҜ , рқ‘Ү . We consider testing the null hypothesis рқҗ» 0 that the sequences {xрқ‘Ў }рқ‘Ў=1,вӢҜ,рқ‘Ү 

and {yрқ‘Ў }рқ‘Ў=1,вӢҜ,рқ‘Ү are independent, against the alternative hypothesis рқҗ» 1 that they are de-pendent. Independence testing in time series has attracted considerable attention in economics, fi-nancial data analysis, machine learning, and biological studies, as evidenced by the grow-ing body of literature, including Kawaller et al. (1987), Shao (2009), Besserve et al. (2013), Huang et al. (2016), Thurman et al. (2019), Wang et al. (2021), GГјnther et al. (2022), Yuan & Shou (2024), Ghoshal & Shao (2026). Several reasons motivate this line of research. For example, Robbins & Fisher (2015) leveraged such testing procedures to investigate the re-lationship between inflation and currency exchange rates. In addition, when a time series comprises many features, practitioners may assess dependence structures among subsets of variables, where independence suggests separation into distinct communities. Furthermore, independence testing aids model simplification Wang et al. (2021). If two time series are found to be independent, they may be modeled separately rather than jointly, leading to a substantial reduction in model complexity. Early contributions, such as Koch & Yang (1986), Hong (1996), Brock et al. (1996), Kulperger & Lockhart (1998), Duchesne & Roy (2003), Shao (2009), primarily consid-ered independence testing for scalar processes, while subsequent studies El Himdi & Roy (1997), Bouhaddioui & Roy (2006), Robbins & Fisher (2015), Wang et al. (2021), Meintanis et al. (2022), Chu (2023) generalized the hypothesis testing problem to multivariate and functional time series. A common approach in the literature вҖ” see, for example, Shao 2(2009), Robbins & Fisher (2015) вҖ” assumes that xрқ‘Ў and yрқ‘Ў follow autoregressive models with independent innovations and reduces the hypothesis testing problem to testing inde-pendence between fitted innovations. Although effective in low-dimensional settings, such an approach faces two challenges in high dimensions or when autocovariance stationarity is violated. First, in high-dimensional settings, consistent estimation of vector autoregres-sive models typically requires structural assumptions such as sparsity or low rank in the coefficient matrices Han et al. (2015), Basu & Michailidis (2015), Krampe et al. (2021), Zhang (2023), Wang et al. (2022), which may be restrictive in practice. Second, when the autocovariance structure is nonstationary, fitting autoregressive models no longer recov-ers the underlying innovations, thus invalidating distributional approximations. Moreover, assessing autocovariance stationarity can be difficult in high dimensions Zhang & Chan (2025). To address these challenges, this manuscript proposes a bimodal weighted-average test statistic to test independence between two high-dimensional time series. The proposed method allows the dimensions рқ‘‘ 1 and рқ‘‘ 2 to exceed the sample size рқ‘Ү , imposes no stationarity assumptions on the autocovariance structure, and does not rely on vector autoregressive modeling. By avoiding these restrictions, the procedure is broadly applicable and robust to model misspecification. The remainder of this manuscript is organized as follows: Section 1.2 reviews related litera-ture. Section 2 introduces the test statistic and a dependent wild bootstrap algorithm that assists statistical inference via computer simulations. Section 3 establishes the asymptotic distribution of the test statistic under рқҗ» 0, and proves the validity of the proposed bootstrap algorithm. Sections 4 and 5 respectively demonstrate the finite-sample performance and the applications of the test through numerical experiments and real data examples. Addi-tional results, including further numerical experiments and detailed proofs of the theoretical 3results, are postponed to the online supplement. 

## 1.2 Related literature 

This section provides a review of the literature on independence testing and its practical ap-plications; further review of methods for analyzing high-dimensional vector autoregressive (VAR) processes is deferred to the online supplement. 

Independence testing. Research on testing the independence of two stationary scalar time series dates back to Haugh (1976), Pierce (1977), with more recent studies including Dalla et al. (2022), Giraitis et al. (2024), Leong (2025). This line of research has been extended to vector time series; see, for example, Bouhaddioui & Roy (2006), Hallin & Saidi (2007), Robbins & Fisher (2015), Wang et al. (2021), Dette & Kroll (2024). We also refer to Meintanis et al. (2022), Zhu et al. (2024) for independence testing in the functional data setting, and to Chen & Liu (2018), Yao et al. (2018), Zhu et al. (2020), Albert et al. (2022), Cai et al. (2024) for independence testing with high-dimensional independent observations. Despite this extensive body of literature, to the best of our knowledge, relatively few studies have been conducted on independence testing for high-dimensional nonstationary time series, where the temporal dependence can affect the null distribution of the test statistic under рқҗ» 0, potentially leading to size inflation. 

Applications. Independence testing serves as a key component in many complex algo-rithms. For example, as shown in Hoyer et al. (2008), Spirtes et al. (2012), Peters et al. (2013), it complements causal discovery methods by determining the direction of causality. In addition, the works of Song et al. (2007, 2012) employ independence testing for feature selection. We also refer readers to Tsonis et al. (2015), Cliff et al. (2021, 2023), Yuan & Shou (2024), Doubovikov & Aksenov (2025), among others, for several real-world data applications. 41.3 Frequently-used notations

52 Setting and methodology 

This section introduces the bimodal weighted-average test statistic, along with a corre-sponding dependent wild bootstrap algorithm for hypothesis testing. 

## 2.1 Constructing the test statistic 

Suppose the observed time series zрқ‘Ў = ( xвҠӨрқ‘Ў , yвҠӨрқ‘Ў )вҠӨ вҲҲ Rрқ‘‘ 1+рқ‘‘ 2 satisfies E [zрқ‘Ў ] = 0, and the objective is to test the independence between the sequences {xрқ‘Ў }рқ‘ЎвҲҲ Z and {yрқ‘Ў }рқ‘ЎвҲҲ Z . Unlike in the independent-data setting Gretton et al. (2007), Albert et al. (2022), the presence of nonzero autocovariances in time series induces bias in the test statistic, inflating its magnitude even under рқҗ» 0. Existing literature, such as Robbins & Fisher (2015), Wang et al. (2021) removed the autocovariances by whitening the data via fitting a vector autoregressive model. However, such methods are generally unsuitable for nonstationary time series. This work attenuates autocovariances by excluding pairs with small time lags. Specifically, for two given integer bandwidths 1 < рқңҶ 1 < рқңҶ 2 < рқ‘Ү and a given lag в„Һ вүҘ 0, we define the test statistic as МӮрқ‘… =

Viewing рқ‘“(вӢ…) as a kernel function, it is even and exhibits a bimodal shape: it vanishes at the origin and for large |рқ‘Ҙ| ,while attaining its maximum at intermediate values in (0, вҲһ). This contrasts with standard kernel functions, which typically peak at zero and decay as |рқ‘Ҙ| increases. In this sense, the proposed test statistic is constructed using a piecewise constant kernel рқ‘“. Remarks 1 and 2 derive the expectations of the test statistic under both рқҗ» 0 and рқҗ» 1, thereby explaining why МӮ рқ‘… distinguishes between temporal dependence and dependence across two time series.

Consequently, the proposed statistic accumulates these nonzero contributions, leading to a non-negligible bias under 7рқҗ» 1. This mechanism allows the test to distinguish cross-series dependence from temporal dependence within each series. Remark 2 also highlights the effect of nonstationarity. If the time series xрқ‘Ў and yрқ‘Ў 

have stationary autocovariances, then О рқ‘Ў,рқ‘ ,1 = О  1,рқ‘ ,1 for all рқ‘Ў, and Tr (О  рқ‘Ў 1,рқ‘ ,1 О вҠӨрқ‘Ў,рқ‘ ,1 ) =

In contrast, without autocovariance stationarity, this bias can be negative, and a two-sided (rather than one-sided) alternative is required, which is different from the tests for stationary time series considered in Robbins & Fisher (2015). Figure 1 plots the dynamics of the bias of the test statistic under both рқҗ» 0 and рқҗ» 1, in agreement with Remarks 1 and 2. Under the null, the bias is driven by the autocovariances, and increasing рқңҶ 1 renders this bias negligible. In contrast, under рқҗ» 1, the presence of nonzero cross-covariance matrices induces extra bias in the test statistic, thereby inflating its absolute value.

Blue dots denote the estimated bias, and red lines indicate the fitted regression line. 82.2 Bootstrap-assisted hypothesis testing 

The complex autocovariance structure of nonstationary time series induces a nontrivial variance for the test statistic. To circumvent explicit variance estimation, we develop a bootstrap procedure that approximates the sampling distribution of the test statistic through simulation. The literature offers a wide range of bootstrap algorithms for statistical inference with time series data. Examples include Politis & Romano (1994), Paparoditis & Politis (2001, 2002), Tewes et al. (2019) for general stationary time series; BГјhlmann (1997), Meyer & Kreiss (2015), Jentsch & Politis (2015) for linear processes; and Zhou (2015), Wu & Zhou (2018) for nonstationary time series. Shao (2010) introduced the dependent wild bootstrap for stationary time series, and subsequent works, such as Zhang & Politis (2023), Zhang et al. (2024), have extended it to accommodate nonstationary settings. The classical dependent wild bootstrap was primarily designed for linear combinations of observations (such as sample mean) and therefore could not fully capture higher-order cumulant information. More recent studies, including Zhang et al. (2024), Zhang (2025), have extended the algorithm to account for fourth-order cumulants. Building on these advances, we employ a second-order dependent wild bootstrap for hypothesis testing. The implementation of the bootstrap algorithm needs a kernel function that satisfies the following conditions. 

Definition 1 (Kernel function) . Suppose the function рқҗҫ(вӢ…) вҲ¶ R вҶ’ [0, вҲһ) satisfies рқҗҫ(0) = 1, рқҗҫ(вӢ…) is decreasing on [0, вҲһ) and is symmetric around 0, and sup рқ‘ҘвҲҲ R |рқҗҫ вҖІ(рқ‘Ҙ)| < вҲһ. 

Assume that вҲ«R рқҗҫ(рқ‘Ҙ) dрқ‘Ҙ < вҲһ. For any рқ‘Ў вҲҲ R, define the Fourier transform of рқҗҫ(вӢ…)

вҲ«R в„ұрқҗҫ(рқ‘Ў) dрқ‘Ў < вҲһ.

Furthermore, the mean and variance of МӮ рқ‘… вҲ—рқ‘Ҹ in the bootstrap world are given by 0 and вҲ‘рқ‘Ү рқ‘Ў 1=1+рқңҶ 1

> рқ’Ұ рқ‘Ү

) re-spectively. Therefore, Algorithm 1 actually generates mean-zero normal random variables with a specific variance. Furthermore, as demonstrated in Theorem 3.3, the test statistic has an asymptotic mean-zero normal distribution under рқҗ» 0. Hence, the distribution of МӮрқ‘… вҲ—рқ‘Ҹ in the bootstrap world approximates that of МӮ рқ‘…, provided that its variance is close to the asymptotic variance of МӮ рқ‘…. This variance consistency is proved in (D.49) of the online supplement. 

Remark 4. The variance of МӮ рқ‘… вҲ—рқ‘Ҹ in the bootstrap world serves as a heteroskedasticity and au-tocorrelation consistent (HAC) estimator of the asymptotic variance of МӮ рқ‘… . HAC estimators, originating in Newey & West (1987), Andrews (1991), have proven useful for heterogeneous data and nonstationary time series; see, for example, Shao (2010), Zhou (2015), Ding & Zhou (2020), Wang & Sun (2022), Sun & Wang (2022), Zhang & Politis (2023). In our set-ting, where no parametric structure is imposed on fourth-order cumulants, HAC estimation provides a natural and flexible approach for estimating variance. 

# 3 Asymptotic results 

This section establishes the asymptotic distribution of the test statistic under рқҗ» 0, as well as the consistency of Algorithm 1. To this end, we introduce a class of short-range depen-dence conditions, named (рқ‘Җ, рқӣј) -short-range dependence, which accommodates nonlinear and nonstationary data-generating processes. Under these conditions, we derive concen-tration inequalities for quadratic forms of high-dimensional time series. These results are of independent interest, as many statistics in the time series literatureвҖ”including sample 11 covariance and precision matrices, spectral density Zhang & Wu (2021), and sample au-toregressive coefficient matrices Guo et al. (2016), Zhang (2023)вҖ”can be reduced to the analysis of quadratic forms. Consequently, the developments in Section 3.1 may be of broader interest beyond the scope of this paper. 

## 3.1 A short-range dependence condition 

Suppose {рқ‘’ рқ‘Ў вҲҲ H, рқ‘Ў вҲҲ Z} are independent (but not necessarily identically distributed) ran-dom variables taking values in H, where H is a measurable space. We assume that 

zрқ‘Ў = рқ‘” рқ‘Ў,рқ‘Ү (вӢҜ , рқ‘’ рқ‘ЎвҲ’2 , рқ‘’ рқ‘ЎвҲ’1 ) , (3.1) where рқ‘” рқ‘Ў,рқ‘Ү (вӢ…) вҲ¶ HZ вҶ’ Rрқ‘‘ 1+рқ‘‘ 2 is a measurable function. for any рқ‘Ў. For any integer рқ‘  вҲҲ Z, define

Definition 2 ((рқ‘Җ, рқӣј) -short-range dependence) . For two given real numbers рқ‘Җ, рқӣј > 1, 

suppose that the random variables zрқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ў вҲҲ Z satisfy equation (3.1). Furthermore, we assume 12 that E [zрқ‘Ў ] = 0, sup рқ‘ЎвҲҲ Z; aвҲҲRрқ‘‘ 1+рқ‘‘ 2 , | a|2=1 вҲҘaвҠӨzрқ‘Ў вҲҘрқ‘Җ = рқ‘Ӯ(1), and sup рқ‘ вүҘ0 (1 + рқ‘ ) рқӣј вҲ‘вҲһрқ‘һ=рқ‘  рқӣҝ рқ‘һ =рқ‘Ӯ(1). Then we say that zрқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ў вҲҲ Z satisfies the (рқ‘Җ, рқӣј) -short-range dependence. 

Remark 5. Discussions of processes of the form (3.1) trace back to a conjecture of N. Wiener, later disproved by Rosenblatt (2009). Wu (2005) introduced a short-range dependence condition for processes of the form (3.1). Subsequent studiesвҖ”such as Shao & Wu (2007), Zhou & Shao (2013), Wu & Wu (2016)вҖ”have applied this condition to derive distributional results for various statistics. However, Wu (2005) assumed that the time series zрқ‘Ў is strictly stationary, i.e., the joint distribution of zрқ‘Ў+1 , вӢҜ , zрқ‘Ў+рқ‘ҳ is identical to the joint distribution of 

z1, вӢҜ , zрқ‘ҳ for any рқ‘ҳ вүҘ 1 and any рқ‘Ў вҲҲ Z, as in Definition 1.3.3 of Brockwell & Davis (1991). More recent studiesвҖ”including Zhou (2013), Wu et al. (2026), Zhang et al. (2024), Zhang & Wu (2017, 2021), Zhang & Cheng (2018)вҖ”have extended the dependence conditions of Wu (2005) to accommodate nonstationary and high-dimensional time series. Definition 2 also generalizes the conditions of Wu (2005) to the setup of high-dimensional nonstationary time series, but it imposes moment bounds on linear combinations рқ‘Һ вҠӨzрқ‘Ў , rather than on individual entries. This formulation is motivated by the test statistic involving inner prod-ucts of observations. Related conditions appeared in Chen & Qin (2010), Zhang (2025), where quadratic-type test statistics were considered. The literature has offered alternative short-range dependence conditions, including vari-ous mixing conditions Doukhan (1994) and near-epoch dependence (NED) Adamek et al. (2023). The process defined in (3.1) can be viewed as a special case of an NED process, where the innovations рқ‘’ рқ‘Ў are independent rather than mixing. As noted in Wu (2005), verifying Definition 2 is comparatively simple relative to mixing conditions, as it only re-quires bounding moments of random variables, whereas mixing conditions typically involve controlling joint probabilities. On the other hand, Definition 2 relies on a nonlinear moving-average representation of the observations, while mixing conditions generally do not impose 13 a specific functional form on the data-generating process.

Therefore, in a heuristic sense, Definition 2 ensures that the temporal correlations of xрқ‘Ў and 

yрқ‘Ў decrease at a polynomial rate. The parameter рқӣј quantifies the strength of dependence: a smaller рқӣј corresponds to a slower decay of covariances, indicating potentially strong dependence. 

## 3.2 Asymptotic distribution under рқҗ» 0

By leveraging concentration inequalities developed in Section 3.1, this section establishes the asymptotic distribution of the test statistic and the validity of the bootstrap algorithm under the null hypothesis, which controls the Type-I error of the test. Theorem 3.3 builds on ideas from Chernozhukov et al. (2013), Zhang & Cheng (2018), Xu et al. (2019) in controlling approximation errors, but differs from these works in that it derives asymptotic distributional approximations for quadratic forms of dependent data. 

Theorem 3.3. Suppose Assumptions D.1, D.2, D.3 of Appendix D.1 hold true. Then we have 

sup 

> рқ‘ҘвҲҲ R

вҲЈpr (МӮрқ‘… вүӨ рқ‘Ҙ) вҲ’ pr (рқң– вүӨ рқ‘Ҙ)вҲЈ = рқ‘ң(1), (3.5) 

where the random variable рқң– has normal distribution with E [рқң–] = 0 and Var (рқң–) = Var (МӮрқ‘… вҖ ) , 

and МӮ рқ‘… вҖ  is the canonical statistic defined in Assumption D.3 of Appendix D.1. 

16 Theorem 3.3 further justifies the claim in Remark 1: For appropriate choices of рқңҶ 1, the test statistic has an asymptotic normal distribution with mean zero. The asymptotic normality also makes it reasonable to sample jointly normal random variables in Algorithm 1. The validity of Algorithm 1 hinges on the fact that the distribution of the bootstrapped statistics МӮ рқ‘… вҲ—рқ‘Ҹ in the bootstrap world accurately approximates the distribution of МӮ рқ‘…. Specif-ically, define the вҖңprobability in the bootstrap worldвҖқвҖ”that is, the conditional probability conditional on all observationsвҖ”as pr вҲ— (вӢ…) = pr (вӢ… вҲЈ zрқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ў = 1, вӢҜ , рқ‘Ү ) . According to Theorem 1.2.1 of Politis et al. (1999), it suffices to establish that sup 

> рқ‘ҘвҲҲ R

вҲЈpr вҲ— (МӮрқ‘… вҲ—рқ‘Ҹ вүӨ рқ‘Ҙ) вҲ’ pr (рқң– вүӨ рқ‘Ҙ)вҲЈ = рқ‘ң рқ‘қ (1), (3.6) where рқң– is defined in Theorem 3.3. We demonstrate this result in Theorem 3.4. 

Theorem 3.4. Suppose Assumptions D.1, D.2, D.3, and D.4 of Appendix D.1 hold true. Then (3.6) holds true. 

# 4 Numerical simulations 

## 4.1 Implementation details 

The proposed testing procedure involves four bandwidth parameters: рқңҶ 1, рқңҶ 2, в„Һ, and рқ’Ұ рқ‘Ү .Among these bandwidths, в„Һ determines the maximal lag up to which mutual dependence is captured; as discussed in Hong (1996), Duchesne & Roy (2003), Shao (2009), Robbins & Fisher (2015). The parameter рқ’Ұ рқ‘Ү corresponds to the bandwidth used in HAC estimation, which is common in the literature; see, for example, Shao (2010), Ding & Zhou (2020), Zhang & Politis (2023). The literature has proposed various methods for selecting рқ’Ұ рқ‘Ү in different kinds of bootstrap settings Hall et al. (1995), Lahiri (2005), Lahiri et al. (2007). In particular, the original dependent wild bootstrap of Shao (2010) leveraged Politis & White (2004) for bandwidth selection. 17 In contrast, рқңҶ 1 and рқңҶ 2 are associated with the proposed test statistic МӮ рқ‘…. The parameter 

рқңҶ 1 serves to mitigate the bias induced by temporal dependence, while рқңҶ 2 controls the dependence strength in the products of time series data. Algorithm 2 provides a data-driven method for selecting рқңҶ 1. We then set рқңҶ 2 = рқң„рқңҶ 1, with different scale parameters рқң„. 

Numerical experiments indicate that the size of the proposed test remains stable across a wide range of рқңҶ 2. However, the power can be affected if рқңҶ 2 is chosen to be excessively small or excessively large. The design of Algorithm 2 is motivated by the need to control the temporal bias of the test statistic. To illustrate this, suppose the null hypothesis holds true and assume that 

zрқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ў вҲҲ Z is weak stationary. From equation (B.1) in the online supplement, if we set в„Һ = 0, 

then

Notably, the choice вҢҠ2рқ‘Ү 0.2 вҢӢ violates Assumption D.4 in the online supplement. We intentionally include this ratio to assess the robustness of the proposed test to violations of the underlying assumptions. Following Robbins & Fisher (2015), we consider three choices of в„Һ = вҢҠ log (рқ‘Ү )вҢӢ, вҢҠ6рқ‘Ү 0.1 вҢӢ, вҢҠ6рқ‘Ү 0.3 вҢӢ. 

This experiment adopts the method proposed in Politis & White (2004), implemented in the R package blocklength , to select рқ’Ұ рқ‘Ү . Since the procedure in Politis & White (2004) is designed for univariate time series, we apply it to the scalar series zвҠӨрқ‘Ў zрқ‘Ў for рқ‘Ў = 1, вҖҰ , рқ‘Ү .

## 4.3 Simulation results 

We choose the sample size рқ‘Ү = 300, 500, 700 and the dimensions рқ‘‘ 1 = 1.2рқ‘Ү , рқ‘‘ 2 = 1.5рқ‘Ү . The numerical results for рқ‘Ү = 500 are reported in Table 1, and other results are postponed to Table 5 of the online supplement. We compare our proposed method with the HSIC-based testing procedure of Wang et al. (2021). As shown in Table 1, the size of the proposed 20 hypothesis testing procedure is insensitive to different choices of рқңҶ 2 and в„Һ. In particular, even when the choices of рқңҶ 2 and в„Һ are chosen to be of higher order than required by Assumption D.3, the test sizes remain close to the nominal level. However, appropriate choices of в„Һ and рқңҶ 2 can improve the power performance. From Table 1, when рқңҶ 2 is set to a moderate level ( вҢҠ2рқ‘Ү 0.1 вҢӢрқңҶ 1 in the experiment), the proposed hypothesis testing procedure achieves the most favorable power performance overall compared to other choices. On the other hand, selecting values of рқңҶ 2 that are either too small or too large leads to a deterioration in power. Numerical results also indicate that adopting a relatively large bandwidth в„Һ is beneficial for improving power performance. Table 1 further highlights the necessity of applying independence tests directly to the time series data zрқ‘Ў , rather than to fitted residuals, in nonstationary settings. The issue arises from potential model incompatibility and is further illustrated by comparison with Table 2, where the data-generating process follows a stationary AR(1) model of the form 

zрқ‘Ў = Az рқ‘ЎвҲ’1 + рқң– рқ‘Ў .

Specifically, when the time series exhibits a stationary autocovariance structure, practi-tioners can employ parametric modelsвҖ”such as vector ARMA processesвҖ”to remove auto-covariances by computing fitted residuals. In contrast, for nonstationary time series with evolving covariance structures, few models are capable of adequately capturing the underly-ing dependence. As a result, model misspecification induces residual autocorrelation, which in turn deteriorates the size and power performance of the HSIC-based testing procedure proposed in Wang et al. (2021). When the data-generating process exhibits nonlinearity, the size of the test remains stable, whereas its power is affected. Table 6 further illustrates that relatively large sample sizes are required to achieve satisfactory power under nonstationarity. These findings suggest the need for developing independence tests that are suitable for capturing nonlinear dependence 21 structures, for example by exploiting reproducing kernel methods as in Gretton et al. (2005), which we leave for future research. 

Necessity of selecting a reasonably large рқңҶ 1. Equation (B.3) suggests that, under рқҗ» 0,

choosing a positive рқңҶ 1 removes the bias induced by temporal dependence. This theoretical implication is further supported by the numerical results in Table 3, where we perform hypothesis testing with different values of рқңҶ 1. When рқңҶ 1 = 0, the bias arising from temporal dependence dominates the stochastic error, causing the test to reject рқҗ» 0 regardless of the underlying hypothesis. When рқңҶ 1 is nonzero but relatively small, the temporal dependence bias still affects the test sizeвҖ”it even partially offsets the bias induced under рқҗ» 1, resulting in power that can be lower than the size. 

# 5 Applications 

This section illustrates the practical use of the proposed test in two applications. The first facilitates causal discovery by refining causal directions after applying causal discovery algorithms (e.g., PC algorithm in Section 5.4.2 of Spirtes et al. (2012)). The second applies the test to financial data to detect cross-sector dependence in stock returns. 

Causal discovery. After observing a time series zрқ‘Ў = ( xвҠӨрқ‘Ў , yвҠӨрқ‘Ў )вҠӨ for рқ‘Ў = 1, вӢҜ , рқ‘Ү , one goal of causal discovery involves determining the direction of causalityвҖ”whether xрқ‘Ў causes yрқ‘Ў 

or yрқ‘Ў causes xрқ‘Ў вҖ”as studied in Hoyer et al. (2008), Huang et al. (2020). Zheng et al. (2024) demonstrated that independence tests could complement causal discovery algorithms, such as the PC algorithm in Spirtes et al. (2012), when causal directions were not identifiable. Hoyer et al. (2008) proposed a three-step procedure for causal discovery: 1. Test if xрқ‘Ў and 

yрқ‘Ў are independent; 2. If independence is rejected, fit the model yрқ‘Ў = f (xрқ‘Ў ) + рқң– рқ‘Ў,1 , estimate МӮ

f, and test independence between xрқ‘Ў and the residuals МӮ рқң– рқ‘Ў,1 = yрқ‘Ў вҲ’МӮ f(xрқ‘Ў ). Failure to reject the null hypothesis suggests the causal direction xрқ‘Ў вҶ’ yрқ‘Ў . 3. We apply the PC algorithm as implemented in Zheng et al. (2024) to recover causal re-lationships among the observations. Since the algorithm is designed for scalar data, we reduce dimensionality at each node via principal component analysis. The resulting graph is displayed in Figure 2b. Due to temporal dependence and information loss during dimen-sion reduction, the original PC algorithm includes a spurious edge and fails to correctly identify causal directions. Following Hoyer et al. (2008), we use Lasso to estimate f and g, and apply Algorithm 1 to calibrate the direction of causality. The results, reported in Table 4 and Figure 2, show that the calibrated directions align with the true causal graph. However, the spurious edge 

рқ‘Ҡ 3 вҶ’ рқ‘Ҡ 2 cannot be removed because of the presence of the common confounder рқ‘Ҡ 1; in this case, regression adjustment fails to eliminate the induced dependence between wрқ‘Ў,3 

and wрқ‘Ў,2 .

The tuning parameters are set to (рқңҶ 1, рқңҶ 2, в„Һ, рқҗҫ рқ‘Ү ) = (2, 6, 9, 7.8). The test results indicate dependence between the communication services and the information technology sectors, which is economically plausible given their business over-lap. For instance, companies such as Alphabet Inc. (communication services) are closely related to firms such as Apple Inc. (information technology) through shared platforms and cloud-based services, as discussed in Cennamo (2021). We also observe dependence among the health care, information technology, and financial sectors. This pattern may reflect the growing integration of cloud computing and AI-driven technologies in health care and financial services, as demonstrated in Athey & Stern (2000), Gu et al. (2020). In empiri-cal studies, detecting dependence across time series provides useful empirical evidence and motivates further investigation into related areas.  

> (a) Observation examples (b) Hypothesis testing results

Figure 3: Observation examples and hypothesis testing results on S&P 500 stock prices. The empirical P-values are computed in the same way as in Figure 2. 27 A Additional literature review 

High-dimensional vector autoregressive (VAR) processes. As demonstrated in Robbins & Fisher (2015), Wang et al. (2021), one possible approach is to whiten the time series by fitting a VAR model and conducting hypothesis testing based on the resulting residuals. However, this approach encounters substantial difficulties in high-dimensional settings. Consistent estimation of VAR coefficient matrices typically relies on structural assumptions, such as sparsity or low-rank conditions; see, for example, Han et al. (2015), Basu & Michailidis (2015), Guo et al. (2016), Basu et al. (2019), Krampe et al. (2023), Zhang (2023), Enikeeva et al. (2025). Furthermore, when the time series lacks a stationary autocovariance structure, which is common in modern applications as demonstrated in Das & Politis (2021), the fitted residuals may fail to approximate the underlying innovations. Consequently, the whitening step can be unreliable, undermining the validity of subsequent independence tests.

.

(B.3) From (B.3), although the test statistic exhibits bias arising from temporal dependence under 

рқҗ» 0, this bias decays at a polynomial rate with respect to the bandwidth рқңҶ 1. By selecting a sufficiently large рқңҶ 1, the bias induced by temporal dependence becomes negligible relative to the stochastic error, and, as demonstrated in Theorem 3.3, the test statistic remains of constant order. 

Remark 7 (Under рқҗ» 1). Due to the dependence across two time series, E [xвҠӨрқ‘Ў xрқ‘Ў 1 yвҠӨрқ‘ЎвҲ’рқ‘  yрқ‘Ў 1вҲ’рқ‘  ] вү

## C.2 Additional numerical experiments 

Table 5 further extends Table 1 in the manuscript by considering different sample sizes. The results show that the empirical size remains stable even when the sample size is relatively small. However, the sample size has a relatively strong effect on the power performance: the power of the test deteriorates for smaller sample sizes. 

Influence of high-dimensionality, dimension imbalance, and data nonlinearity. 

Table 6 further assesses the effects of high dimensionality, dimensional imbalance, and the nonlinear mutual dependence structure on the performance of the testing procedure. The results show that both size and power remain stable when the dimension is large relative to the sample size. However, significant dimensional imbalance can lead to a loss of power. In addition, when nonlinear dependence is present, a relatively large sample size is required to maintain satisfactory power. 31 Table 5: Size and power performance of the proposed test procedure. The sample sizes are 300 and 700, and the nominal size of the test is 5%. We repeat the experiment 100 times to evaluate the size and power.

вҲЈрқҗө рқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈ2рқҗ№ = рқҗ¶ вҲҡвҲҡвҲҡвҺ·

> рқ‘Ү

вҲ‘

> рқ‘Ў 1=1+рқңҶ 1
> рқ‘Ў 1вҲ’рқңҶ 1

вҲ‘

> рқ‘Ў 2=(рқ‘Ў 1вҲ’рқңҶ 2)вҲЁ1

вҲЈAрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈ2рқҗ№ .

We derive the moment bound in (3.3) by further employing the inequality вҲЈAрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈрқҗ№ вүӨвҲҡрқ‘‘ вҲЈ Aрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈ2 . As a result, we adopt a relatively loose moment bound to compensate for the effect of temporal dependence in the proof. However, the inequality вҲЈAрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈрқҗ№ вүӨ вҲҡрқ‘‘ вҲЈ Aрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈ2

does not change the order of the moment bound: If all singular values of Aрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 are of the same order, then вҲЈAрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈрқҗ№ вүҚ вҲҡрқ‘‘ вҲЈ Aрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈ2 . Since our primary interest lies in the order of the moment bound, replacing вҲЈAрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈрқҗ№ with вҲҡрқ‘‘ вҲЈ Aрқ‘Ў 1рқ‘Ў 2 вҲЈ2 does not lead to too much information loss. We then present a corollary of Theorem 3.1 that serves an important role in the proof of Theorem 3.4.

From (D.19), (D.20), (D.27), (D.31), (D.32), (D.33), we prove (3.5). We then prove the validity of the bootstrap algorithm 1, which is summarized in Theorem 3.4. Since Algorithm 1 samples joint normal random variables and constructs the bootstrap statistic as a linear combination of these sampled normal random variables, its validity actually relies on the fact that the variance of the bootstrap statistic in the bootstrap world closely approximates that of the estimator МӮ рқ‘…, which is proved below. 68 Assumption D.4. Define рқӣј and рқң… рқ‘§ , рқ‘§ = 1, 2, в„Һ as those in Assumption D.1 and Assumption D.2. Assume that рқӣј > 17 and 1/6 > рқң… 2 > рқң… 1 > 52(рқӣјвҲ’2) and 1/6 > рқң… в„Һ > 0. Suppose the bandwidth рқ’Ұ рқ‘Ү вүҚ рқ‘Ү рқң… рқ‘ҳ , where 1/2 вҲ’ (рқң… 2 вҲЁ рқң… в„Һ) > рқң… рқ‘ҳ > 2(рқң… 2 вҲЁ рқң… в„Һ). 

Assumption D.4 imposes a stronger condition than Assumption D.1 and Assumption D.2. This trade-off is required for the consistent estimation of the variance of the estimator МӮ рқ‘….
